The Ādāb al-Baḥth Genre: Origins and Development
Editor's note on the founder of Ādāb al-Baḥth and Samarqandi's commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the origins of the Ādāb al-Baḥth genre and its relationship to jadal.

Core Idea
Ishaq al-Shashi wrote the first book on the rules of discussion, founding a genre that Samarqandi later developed.

The editor's introduction identifies Ishaq al-Shashi as the first to write on Ādāb al-Baḥth. This lesson explores the emergence of this discipline, its connection to earlier dialectical traditions, and how Samarqandi's commentary contributed to its development.

Guided Path
1
Identify the founder of the Ādāb al-Baḥth genre
Find in the passage the scholar Ishaq bin Ibrahim al-Shashi (d. 325 AH) and note that he is credited as the first author of a work on the rules of discussion and debate.
This is explicitly stated in the text.
2
Understand the relationship between Ādāb al-Baḥth and jadal
Notice that Samarqandi wrote works on both Ādāb al-Baḥth and jadal. The passage lists several titles on jadal, indicating that the two genres are closely related but distinct.
In later tradition, Ādāb al-Baḥth becomes a separate discipline focusing on the conduct of debate.
3
Examine Samarqandi's commentary on al-Shashi's epistle
The main text introduced is a commentary on al-Shashi's epistle. This shows that Samarqandi engaged with and expanded upon the earlier work.

Editor's introduction. About Samarqandi 11. [First edition] printed in Cairo in 1353 AH (1934 CE) edited by Mahmud al-Imam al-Mansuri, and second edition in 1435 AH (2014 CE) edited by Izza Abd al-Rahman Ahmad Abd al-Rahman al-Nadi. Muhammad bin Muhammad Khabushani; written 763 AH in Madina Dar al-Salam. Hamid al-Din al-Shashi, eighth century [AH]. Muhammad bin Shahrukh Ulugh Beg (d. 853 AH). Ala al-Din Ali bin Muhammad Shahroudi Bistami known as Musannifak (d. 875 AH). Kamal al-Din Masud Muhaqqiq al-Shirwani (d. 905 AH/1499 CE). Qutb al-Din Muhammad al-Gilani who wrote his commentary in 891 AH/1486 CE. Abd al-Latif bin Abd al-Mu'min bin Ishaq, name of the commentary Kashf al-Abkar fi 'Ilm al-Afkar. Burhan al-Din Ibrahim bin Yusuf al-Bulghari. Husayn bin Mu'in al-Din al-Yazdi al-Mibdi (d. 910 AH/1504 CE). On the commentary of Kamal al-Din al-Shirwani, many glosses have been written, and Allamah al-Dawani is the most famous glossator of this commentary. Below are the names of the glosses on this commentary and their authors briefly: Ahmad Junayd; Amir Hasan al-Rumi; Abd al-Rahim al-Shirwani; Abd al-Mu'min al-Barziti known as Nahari Zada (d. 960 AH/1456 CE); Jalal al-Din Muhammad bin As'ad al-Siddiqi al-Dawani (d. 1502/1908 CE); Isam al-Din Ibrahim bin Muhammad al-Isfarayini (died in Samarqand in 943 AH/1536 CE); Muhammad bin Abi Sa'id Taj al-Sa'idi (d. 950 AH). Ahmad Dikfuz, a scholar contemporary with Sultan Muhammad Fatih and among his courtiers. Ala al-Din Ali bin Muhammad known as Musannifak (d. 871 AH/1466 CE), date of writing: 826 AH/1423 CE. Imad al-Din Yahya bin Ahmad al-Kashi, tenth century AH, a commentary known as 'al-Hashiya al-Sawda' due to its very intricate style. [Page 27] 12 About Samarqandi. The commentary on Adab al-Bahth. Editor's Introduction. This book is a commentary on the epistle Adab al-Bahth by Ishaq bin Ibrahim al-Shashi (d. 325 AH), who is apparently the first person to write a book on the rules of discussion and debate. Miftah al-Nazar fi Sharh 'Muqaddima fi al-Jadal wa al-Khilaf wa al-Nazar' authored 690 AH. This book is a commentary on the introduction of one of the books of Burhan al-Din al-Nasafi on dialectics and debate. The next epistle is the following epistle which Walter Edward Young recently edited and published online. 'Ayn al-Nazar fi 'Ilm al-Jadal (2019 Samarqandi). Other independent books of Samarqandi in this field: al-Munyah wa al-Amal fi 'Ilm al-Jadal, Gha'ib al-Nazar fi 'Ilm al-Jadal. About these two works we do not know much, and it is even possible that they are one work or the same as the fourth. Philosophical-Kalam works: From the philosophical-kalam works of Samarqandi, the following can be mentioned: al-Saha'if al-Ilahiyya (1985 CE, Kuwait, edited by Ahmad Abd al-Rahman al-Sharif; 2007 CE, Beirut, edited by Ahmad Farid al-Mazidi); al-Ma'arif fi Sharh al-Saha'if (2015 CE, presented by Nazir Muhammad al-Nazir, translated and edited by Abdullah Muhammad Abdullah, al-Maktaba al-Azhariyya li al-Turath); al-Anwar al-Ilahiyya; Sharh al-Anwar al-Ilahiyya; Bayan Madhahib Ahl al-Sunna; Tahqiq Kalimat al-Shahada. Dadkhah 1392 p. 29.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction to the book Qustas, providing a detailed biographical and bibliographical overview of the 8th/14th century scholar Shams al-Din al-Samarqandi (not to be confused with the earlier scholar Abu al-Qasim al-Samarqandi). The editor begins by noting the publication history of this edition, including the first Cairo edition (1353 AH) and a second edition (1435 AH). He then lists a chain of scholars who worked on Samarqandi's texts, such as Muhammad bin Muhammad Khabushani (who wrote in 763 AH), Hamid al-Din al-Shashi, Ulugh Beg (the Timurid ruler), and the commentator known as Musannifak. The focus shifts to the extensive commentary tradition on Samarqandi's works on logic and dialectics, particularly the commentary by Kamal al-Din al-Shirwani, which attracted many glosses (hawashi). The most famous glosses are those of Jalal al-Din al-Dawani, along with others like Isam al-Din al-Isfarayini and 'Imad al-Din al-Kashi (whose gloss is known as 'al-Hashiya al-Sawda' or the Black Gloss due to its dense style). The editor then identifies the main text being introduced: the commentary on Adab al-Bahth by Ishaq al-Shashi, who is credited as the founder of the genre of disputation theory. Other works by Samarqandi on jadal (dialectics) are listed: Miftah al-Nazar, 'Ayn al-Nazar, al-Munyah wa al-Amal, and Gha'ib al-Nazar, with a note of uncertainty about whether the last two are distinct works. Finally, Samarqandi's philosophical and kalam works are listed, including al-Saha'if al-Ilahiyya and its commentary, al-Anwar al-Ilahiyya, and treatises on Sunni doctrine and the testimony of faith. Throughout, the passage functions as a scholarly apparatus, orienting the reader to the textual history and the key figures in the reception of Samarqandi's logical and dialectical corpus.

الجدل

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Commentary Tradition on Samarqandi's Logical Works
Editor's list of glossators on Shirwani's commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key commentators and glossators on Samarqandi's logical works and understand the significance of the gloss tradition.

Core Idea
The many glosses on Samarqandi's works reflect a vibrant scholarly tradition of refinement and debate.

The editor's introduction lists numerous scholars who wrote glosses (hawashi) on the commentary of Kamal al-Din al-Shirwani on Samarqandi's works. This lesson examines the tradition of super-commentaries and its role in the transmission and critical development of Samarqandi's ideas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main commentary that attracted glosses
The passage states that 'on the commentary of Kamal al-Din al-Shirwani, many glosses have been written'. This is the central text for the gloss tradition.
2
List the most famous glossators
The passage mentions Jalal al-Din al-Dawani as the most famous, along with others like 'Isam al-Din al-Isfarayini and 'Imad al-Din al-Kashi.
Al-Kashi's gloss is known as 'al-Hashiya al-Sawda' due to its complexity.
3
Understand the purpose of glosses
Glosses (hawashi) are super-commentaries that provide critical refinements, explanations, or objections. They show the active engagement of later scholars with Samarqandi's logic.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Samarqandi's Intellectual Project: Logic, Theology, and Science
Editor surveys Samarqandi's works in logic, theology, and natural sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Samarqandi's major contributions to logic, theology, and natural sciences and explain why his analysis of conditional syllogisms is considered innovative.

Core Idea
Samarqandi's works map the intersection of logic, theology, and natural sciences in the Islamic tradition.

This lesson orients you to the author of Qustas al-Afkar, Shams al-Din al-Samarqandi, whose innovations in conditional syllogisms are central to the text. The editor's introduction surveys his writings across logic (including jadal), theology (kalam), and natural sciences. Understanding this breadth helps you appreciate why Samarqandi's logical work was groundbreaking and how it fits within the wider Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Survey the passage structure
Notice that the editor groups Samarqandi's works into three categories: logic and dialectic (e.g., Adab al-Bahth, Miftah al-Nazar), philosophical theology (e.g., al-Saha'if al-Ilahiyya), and natural/mathematical sciences (e.g., 'Ilm al-Afaq wa al-Anfus). This classification reflects the scope of Samarqandi's interests.
2
Identify the logical works
Locate the titles that explicitly deal with logic and debate: Sharh Adab al-Bahth, Miftah al-Nazar, 'Ayn al-Nazar, al-Munya, Ghayb al-Nazar. These show Samarqandi's deep engagement with the tradition of jadal and adab al-bahth.
Qustas al-Afkar itself is a commentary on Adab al-Bahth by al-Shashi.
3
Find the mention of innovation
The editor notes that Samarqandi's analysis of 'conditional conjunctive syllogisms' (al-qiyasat al-iqtiraniyya al-shartiyya) and his treatment of the 'particular conditional implicative' (sharti muttasil lazumi juz'i) are innovative. This is a key logical contribution that the Qustas develops.
This innovation is a specific technical refinement in syllogistic theory.
4
Connect to the broader Islamic sciences
Realise that Samarqandi did not work in isolation: his logical works interact with theology (as in al-Saha'if al-Ilahiyya) and natural philosophy. This interdisciplinary approach was typical of the mature Islamic tradition.
For example, logical tools were used to structure theological proofs.

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction to the book Qustas, providing a biographical and bibliographical overview of the author, Samarqandi (Shams al-Din al-Samarqandi). The editor begins by stating that this book is a commentary on the treatise Adab al-Bahth by Ishaq al-Shashi, who is credited as the first to write on the etiquette of debate. The editor then lists several other works by Samarqandi on dialectic (jadal), including Miftah al-Nazar, 'Ayn al-Nazar, and others, noting uncertainty about some. The passage proceeds to enumerate Samarqandi's philosophical-theological works, such as al-Saha'if al-Ilahiyya and its commentaries, followed by natural-mathematical-astronomical works like 'Ilm al-Afaq wa al-Anfus and Ashkal al-Ta'sis. The editor laments that many of these works remain unpublished or inaccessible. Finally, the editor highlights Samarqandi's innovations in the field of logic, particularly his analysis of conditional syllogisms, promising that detailed study would require multiple articles. The passage serves as a preliminary orientation to the author and his intellectual contributions.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
6 min
Samarqandi's Expansion of the Subject of Logic
Innovation on subject of logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Ibn Sina's restriction of logic to second intelligibles and Samarqandi's extension to third and fourth intelligibles as predicates of logical problems.

Core Idea
Samarqandi extends logic beyond second intelligibles to include third and fourth as predicates.

In the Islamic logical tradition, Ibn Sina defined the subject of logic as second intelligibles. However, Samarqandi in his Qustas al-Afkar proposed a significant expansion. He argued that the predicates of the problems of logic include third and fourth intelligibles. This lesson examines this innovation and its implications for the scope of logical inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Sina's view
Recall that for Ibn Sina, the subject of logic is exclusively second intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). These are concepts derived from primary intelligibles and are the tools for reasoning.
Second intelligibles are like 'subject', 'predicate', 'universal'.
2
Identify Samarqandi's addition
Samarqandi states that the predicate of the problems of logic includes third and fourth intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thalitha wa al-rabi'a). These are further levels of abstraction.
Third intelligibles are predicates of second intelligibles, fourth are predicates of third, etc.
3
Understand the shift
This expands the domain of logic. Now logical investigation covers not only the tools of reasoning (second intelligibles) but also higher-order concepts derived from them.
This innovation opens logic to meta‑logical analysis.
4
Evaluate implications
Consider how this affects the demarcation of logic from other sciences. If third intelligibles are included, logic becomes more abstract and comprehensive.

Editor's Introduction
13. Samarqandi's innovations: He has written an article and from his own perspective has reported and evaluated his views and analyses (Fallahi 1393, 1397). Very innovative and sometimes contradictory statements that Samarqandi says about veridical (haqiqiyya), external (kharijiyya), and mental (dhihniyya) propositions, and for the first and last time in the history of logic in the Islamic world, he deals with mixed syllogisms of these three types of propositions - requires independent research.
In this short introduction, we will suffice with a list of Samarqandi's innovations in Qustas al-Afkar and leave their detailed and case-by-case examination to the future and future generations.
Section on Concepts (Tasawwurat)
Subject of Logic
. The third and fourth intelligibles in addition to the first and second intelligibles. The subject of logic = second intelligibles (Ibn Sina)
The predicate of the problems of logic = third and fourth intelligibles, etc. (Samarqandi)
The fallacy of the absolutely unknown (shubha al-majhul al-mutlaq)
Solution of the fallacy by distinguishing between essential (dhatiyya) and descriptive (wasfiyya) propositions (possibly influenced by al-Abhari in Khulasat al-Afkar and Naqawat al-Asrar p. 100 lines 14-16)
Topics of Signification (Mabahith al-Dalala)
Definition of signifier (dal) and signification (dalala)
Division of signification into conventional (wad'i), intellectual ('aqli), and natural (tabi'i) (= conventional, sensory, and conjectural)
The conventional nature of the abandonment of implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam)
The four relations (al-nisab al-arba'a)
.6 A new answer to Katibi's puzzle based on the use of 'adami' and 'wujudi' instead of 'ma'dul' and 'muhassal' following Fakhr al-Razi and al-Khunaji
Denial of the condition of continuity (dawam) and actuality (fi'liyya) in the four relations

Editor's Introduction on Universal Natural Kind (Kulli Tabi'i)
Denial of the existence of the universal natural kind in the external world
9. Dealing with the parts of predicate and non-predicate
10. Distinction between external and intellectual composite (murakkab khariji and 'aqli)
.11 Division of composite (murakkab) into real (haqiqi) and conventional ('i'tibari)
Fallacy concerning whether the composition of man (insan) is real or conventional
12. Answer to Fakhr al-Razi's objection on general knowledge ('ilm ijmail)
13. Attribution of formal fallacy (mugalata suriyya) to Fakhr al-Razi
Genus (jins)
Innovations of Samarqandi 15
14. New answers to objections to the definition of genus
15. Defense of genus being a genus for the highest, lowest, intermediate, and individual genera
Categories (Maqulat)
16. Following the Stoics in restricting the categories to four categories
.17 Answer to the objection to the categories being genera
Differentia (fasl)
18. Critique of the definition of 'fasl' in Ibn Sina's Isharaat and the critique of al-Khunaji and al-Urmawi
.19 A new criticism on the causality of the differentia for the specific part of the genus based on Aristotle's view that the body is a condition for the origination of the soul from the active intellect
20. Opposition to Fakhr al-Razi in the differentia being an attribute in real quiddities (mahiyyat haqiqiyya)
Definition (Hadd)
.21 Denial of incomplete definition being the differentia alone 22 Denial of incomplete description being the property (khassa) alone 23 Acceptance of incomplete definition being the combination of differentia and property
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction to a critical edition of Samarqandi's Qustas al-Afkar. It begins by noting Samarqandi's innovative and sometimes contradictory statements regarding veridical, external, and mental propositions, and his unique treatment of mixed syllogisms involving these three types. The editor then states that this introduction will simply list the innovations, leaving detailed analysis for later. The list is organized under headings: Section on Concepts (Mabahith al-Tasawwurat), Topics of Signification (Mabahith al-Dalala), Editor's Introduction on Universal Natural Kind, Genus, Categories, Differentia, and Definition. Under each, specific innovations are enumerated, such as Samarqandi's view that the predicate of logic includes third and fourth intelligibles (not just second), his solution to the fallacy of the absolutely unknown by distinguishing essential and descriptive propositions, his division of signification into three types, and his denial of the condition of continuity and actuality in the four relations. Later sections cover his denial of the existence of the universal natural kind, his distinction between real and conventional composites, his defense of genus for all levels, his following of the Stoics in restricting categories to four, his critique of Ibn Sina's definition of differentia, and his acceptance that incomplete definition can be a combination of differentia and property. The passage serves as a roadmap to the unique logical positions of Samarqandi.

معقولات ثانیه

Key Takeaway

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
6 min
Samarqandi on Signification: Abandonment of Implicative Signification
Innovation on implicative signification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Samarqandi's view that implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam) is conventional and can be abandoned.

Core Idea
Samarqandi breaks with tradition: implicative signification is conventional, not necessary.

In classical logic, signification was often divided into three types: conventional, intellectual, and natural. Implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam) was typically considered necessary. Samarqandi argues that it is conventional and can be abandoned. This lesson explores this innovation and its impact on the theory of meaning.

Guided Path
1
Recall types of signification
Standard division includes wad'i (conventional), 'aqli (intellectual), and tabi'i (natural). Implicative signification is where one thing implies another by association.
Example: smoke implies fire is an intellectual signification.
2
Identify Samarqandi's claim
He states: 'the conventional nature of the abandonment of implicative signification' (قراردادی بودن مهجوریت دلالت التزام). This means implicative signification is based on convention and can be dropped.
This is a shift from earlier logicians who treated it as necessary.
3
Understand the implication
If implicative signification is conventional, then it can vary between languages or contexts, undermining its universality.
This has consequences for legal reasoning and interpretation.
4
Contrast with previous views
Earlier logicians like Ibn Sina and al-Farabi generally considered implicative signification as a real relation independent of convention.

در این مقدمه کوتاه صرفا به فهرستی از نوآوریهای سمرقندی در قسطاس الأفكار بسنده خواهیم کرد و بررسی تفصیلی و موردی آنها را به آینده و آیندگان خواهیم سپرد.

Editor's Introduction
13. Samarqandi's innovations: He has written an article and from his own perspective has reported and evaluated his views and analyses (Fallahi 1393, 1397). Very innovative and sometimes contradictory statements that Samarqandi says about veridical (haqiqiyya), external (kharijiyya), and mental (dhihniyya) propositions, and for the first and last time in the history of logic in the Islamic world, he deals with mixed syllogisms of these three types of propositions - requires independent research.
In this short introduction, we will suffice with a list of Samarqandi's innovations in Qustas al-Afkar and leave their detailed and case-by-case examination to the future and future generations.
Section on Concepts (Tasawwurat)
Subject of Logic
. The third and fourth intelligibles in addition to the first and second intelligibles. The subject of logic = second intelligibles (Ibn Sina)
The predicate of the problems of logic = third and fourth intelligibles, etc. (Samarqandi)
The fallacy of the absolutely unknown (shubha al-majhul al-mutlaq)
Solution of the fallacy by distinguishing between essential (dhatiyya) and descriptive (wasfiyya) propositions (possibly influenced by al-Abhari in Khulasat al-Afkar and Naqawat al-Asrar p. 100 lines 14-16)
Topics of Signification (Mabahith al-Dalala)
Definition of signifier (dal) and signification (dalala)
Division of signification into conventional (wad'i), intellectual ('aqli), and natural (tabi'i) (= conventional, sensory, and conjectural)
The conventional nature of the abandonment of implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam)
The four relations (al-nisab al-arba'a)
.6 A new answer to Katibi's puzzle based on the use of 'adami' and 'wujudi' instead of 'ma'dul' and 'muhassal' following Fakhr al-Razi and al-Khunaji
Denial of the condition of continuity (dawam) and actuality (fi'liyya) in the four relations

Editor's Introduction on Universal Natural Kind (Kulli Tabi'i)
Denial of the existence of the universal natural kind in the external world
9. Dealing with the parts of predicate and non-predicate
10. Distinction between external and intellectual composite (murakkab khariji and 'aqli)
.11 Division of composite (murakkab) into real (haqiqi) and conventional ('i'tibari)
Fallacy concerning whether the composition of man (insan) is real or conventional
12. Answer to Fakhr al-Razi's objection on general knowledge ('ilm ijmail)
13. Attribution of formal fallacy (mugalata suriyya) to Fakhr al-Razi
Genus (jins)
Innovations of Samarqandi 15
14. New answers to objections to the definition of genus
15. Defense of genus being a genus for the highest, lowest, intermediate, and individual genera
Categories (Maqulat)
16. Following the Stoics in restricting the categories to four categories
.17 Answer to the objection to the categories being genera
Differentia (fasl)
18. Critique of the definition of 'fasl' in Ibn Sina's Isharaat and the critique of al-Khunaji and al-Urmawi
.19 A new criticism on the causality of the differentia for the specific part of the genus based on Aristotle's view that the body is a condition for the origination of the soul from the active intellect
20. Opposition to Fakhr al-Razi in the differentia being an attribute in real quiddities (mahiyyat haqiqiyya)
Definition (Hadd)
.21 Denial of incomplete definition being the differentia alone 22 Denial of incomplete description being the property (khassa) alone 23 Acceptance of incomplete definition being the combination of differentia and property
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction to a critical edition of Samarqandi's Qustas al-Afkar. It begins by noting Samarqandi's innovative and sometimes contradictory statements regarding veridical, external, and mental propositions, and his unique treatment of mixed syllogisms involving these three types. The editor then states that this introduction will simply list the innovations, leaving detailed analysis for later. The list is organized under headings: Section on Concepts (Mabahith al-Tasawwurat), Topics of Signification (Mabahith al-Dalala), Editor's Introduction on Universal Natural Kind, Genus, Categories, Differentia, and Definition. Under each, specific innovations are enumerated, such as Samarqandi's view that the predicate of logic includes third and fourth intelligibles (not just second), his solution to the fallacy of the absolutely unknown by distinguishing essential and descriptive propositions, his division of signification into three types, and his denial of the condition of continuity and actuality in the four relations. Later sections cover his denial of the existence of the universal natural kind, his distinction between real and conventional composites, his defense of genus for all levels, his following of the Stoics in restricting categories to four, his critique of Ibn Sina's definition of differentia, and his acceptance that incomplete definition can be a combination of differentia and property. The passage serves as a roadmap to the unique logical positions of Samarqandi.

Key Terms
دلالة التزام
مهجوریت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is Samarqandi's position on implicative signification?
A) It is necessary and cannot be abandoned
B) It is conventional and can be abandoned
C) It is natural and universal
D) It is intellectual and fixed

Key Takeaway
Samarqandi innovates by declaring implicative signification conventional, not necessary, thus allowing it to be abandoned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a case in usul al-fiqh where a linguist claims a necessary implication (e.g., 'the imperative implies obligation'). Determine whether Samarqandi would accept this as necessary or conventional, and justify.
?
Suppose a Quranic verse says 'Establish prayer' in a context of war. Using Samarqandi's view, could one argue that the implication of obligation is abandoned? Why or why not?

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
7 min
Samarqandi's Defense of Genus for All Levels
Innovation on genus applicability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Samarqandi's defense that genus serves as genus for highest, lowest, intermediate, and individual genera.

Core Idea
Samarqandi defends the genus as genus for all levels, from highest to individual.

In logic, the concept of genus (jins) typically applies to categories like 'Animal' for 'Human'. But can 'Animal' be genus for 'Human' as well as for 'Rational Animal'? Samarqandi answers yes, defending the genus's applicability to highest, lowest, intermediate, and even individual genera. This lesson unpacks his innovation.

Guided Path
1
Recall standard definition
Genus is a universal that is predicated of many things differing in species (e.g., 'Animal' for 'Human' and 'Horse').
2
Identify the problem
Objection: If genus is for species, how can it also be for highest genera (which have no higher genus) or for individuals?
3
Samarqandi's defense
He argues that genus can be predicated of all levels: highest (e.g., Substance), lowest (e.g., Animal), intermediate (e.g., Living Being), and even individual (e.g., this human) in a broader sense.
This is a new answer to traditional objections.
4
Evaluate the argument
Consider whether this stretches the definition too far. What does it mean for an individual to have a genus? Possibly as a 'partial' genus.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Samarqandi's Critique of the Definition of Differentia
Innovation on differentia definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Samarqandi's critique of Ibn Sina's definition of differentia (fasl) and his own position on its causality.

Core Idea
Samarqandi offers a new critique of the differentia and its role in defining species.

The differentia (fasl) is a key component in definitions. Ibn Sina defined it as what distinguishes a species within its genus. Samarqandi not only critiques Ibn Sina's definition but also objects to the claim that the differentia is a cause of the specific part of the genus. This lesson explores his innovative criticism.

Guided Path
1
Recall standard definition
Differentia is the logical part that distinguishes one species from another under the same genus (e.g., 'rational' for human).
2
Identify Ibn Sina's definition
In al-Isharat, Ibn Sina defines fasl as what makes a species specifically what it is.
3
Samarqandi's critique
He argues that this definition is inadequate and raises a new criticism: the differentia cannot be a cause (illa) for the specific part of the genus because the body is a condition for the soul's origination, per Aristotle.
This introduces a metaphysical argument.
4
Contrast with Fakhr al-Razi
Samarqandi also opposes Fakhr al-Razi's view that differentia is an attribute in real quiddities.

Source Text

.۲۱ انکار حد ناقص بودن فصل به تنهایی ۲۲ انکار رسم ناقص بودن خاصه به تنهایی ۲۳ پذیرش حد ناقص بودن ترکیب فصل و خاصه
Editor's Introduction
13. Samarqandi's innovations: He has written an article and from his own perspective has reported and evaluated his views and analyses (Fallahi 1393, 1397). Very innovative and sometimes contradictory statements that Samarqandi says about veridical (haqiqiyya), external (kharijiyya), and mental (dhihniyya) propositions, and for the first and last time in the history of logic in the Islamic world, he deals with mixed syllogisms of these three types of propositions - requires independent research.
In this short introduction, we will suffice with a list of Samarqandi's innovations in Qustas al-Afkar and leave their detailed and case-by-case examination to the future and future generations.
Section on Concepts (Tasawwurat)
Subject of Logic
. The third and fourth intelligibles in addition to the first and second intelligibles. The subject of logic = second intelligibles (Ibn Sina)
The predicate of the problems of logic = third and fourth intelligibles, etc. (Samarqandi)
The fallacy of the absolutely unknown (shubha al-majhul al-mutlaq)
Solution of the fallacy by distinguishing between essential (dhatiyya) and descriptive (wasfiyya) propositions (possibly influenced by al-Abhari in Khulasat al-Afkar and Naqawat al-Asrar p. 100 lines 14-16)
Topics of Signification (Mabahith al-Dalala)
Definition of signifier (dal) and signification (dalala)
Division of signification into conventional (wad'i), intellectual ('aqli), and natural (tabi'i) (= conventional, sensory, and conjectural)
The conventional nature of the abandonment of implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam)
The four relations (al-nisab al-arba'a)
.6 A new answer to Katibi's puzzle based on the use of 'adami' and 'wujudi' instead of 'ma'dul' and 'muhassal' following Fakhr al-Razi and al-Khunaji
Denial of the condition of continuity (dawam) and actuality (fi'liyya) in the four relations

Editor's Introduction on Universal Natural Kind (Kulli Tabi'i)
Denial of the existence of the universal natural kind in the external world
9. Dealing with the parts of predicate and non-predicate
10. Distinction between external and intellectual composite (murakkab khariji and 'aqli)
.11 Division of composite (murakkab) into real (haqiqi) and conventional ('i'tibari)
Fallacy concerning whether the composition of man (insan) is real or conventional
12. Answer to Fakhr al-Razi's objection on general knowledge ('ilm ijmail)
13. Attribution of formal fallacy (mugalata suriyya) to Fakhr al-Razi
Genus (jins)
Innovations of Samarqandi 15
14. New answers to objections to the definition of genus
15. Defense of genus being a genus for the highest, lowest, intermediate, and individual genera
Categories (Maqulat)
16. Following the Stoics in restricting the categories to four categories
.17 Answer to the objection to the categories being genera
Differentia (fasl)
18. Critique of the definition of 'fasl' in Ibn Sina's Isharaat and the critique of al-Khunaji and al-Urmawi
.19 A new criticism on the causality of the differentia for the specific part of the genus based on Aristotle's view that the body is a condition for the origination of the soul from the active intellect
20. Opposition to Fakhr al-Razi in the differentia being an attribute in real quiddities (mahiyyat haqiqiyya)
Definition (Hadd)
.21 Denial of incomplete definition being the differentia alone 22 Denial of incomplete description being the property (khassa) alone 23 Acceptance of incomplete definition being the combination of differentia and property
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction to a critical edition of Samarqandi's Qustas al-Afkar. It begins by noting Samarqandi's innovative and sometimes contradictory statements regarding veridical, external, and mental propositions, and his unique treatment of mixed syllogisms involving these three types. The editor then states that this introduction will simply list the innovations, leaving detailed analysis for later. The list is organized under headings: Section on Concepts (Mabahith al-Tasawwurat), Topics of Signification (Mabahith al-Dalala), Editor's Introduction on Universal Natural Kind, Genus, Categories, Differentia, and Definition. Under each, specific innovations are enumerated, such as Samarqandi's view that the predicate of logic includes third and fourth intelligibles (not just second), his solution to the fallacy of the absolutely unknown by distinguishing essential and descriptive propositions, his division of signification into three types, and his denial of the condition of continuity and actuality in the four relations. Later sections cover his denial of the existence of the universal natural kind, his distinction between real and conventional composites, his defense of genus for all levels, his following of the Stoics in restricting categories to four, his critique of Ibn Sina's definition of differentia, and his acceptance that incomplete definition can be a combination of differentia and property. The passage serves as a roadmap to the unique logical positions of Samarqandi.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
Navigating Samarqandi's Logical Innovations
List of topics and innovations by Samarqandi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main topics in Samarqandi's Qustas and explain the significance of the 'positive negative predicate' innovation.

Core Idea
Samarqandi's table of contents is a roadmap to his logical innovations.

The Qustas by Samarqandi is a work on logic that contains numerous innovations commenting on earlier scholars. This lesson focuses on the table of contents, which lists the topics and highlights where Samarqandi breaks new ground. By understanding this outline, you will gain a bird's-eye view of the work's structure and be able to locate specific discussions, such as the controversial 'positive negative predicate' (mūjiba sāliba al-maḥmūl) used in contraposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall structure
Scan the list and note the progression from general logical concepts (universal natural, composite) to specific innovations (genus, categories, differentia, definition) and then to propositions and the positive negative predicate.
The list also includes responses to objections and critiques of earlier logicians like Ibn Sina and Fakhr al-Razi.
2
Spot the innovations
Look for phrases like 'Samarqandi's innovations' or 'new critique'. Note the explicit claims, such as following the Stoics on categories, denying that differentia alone can be a deficient definition, and introducing the positive negative predicate.
Innovations are often signaled by contrast with previous views (e.g., 'opposition to Fakhr al-Razi').
3
Analyze the positive negative predicate entry
Read the detailed note on 'positive negative predicate'. Understand that Samarqandi uses it to distinguish between a universal negative (كل ج ليس ب) and a modified affirmative (كل ج هو ليس ب). This distinction solves problems in contraposition.
The term originated with Khunaji, but Samarqandi refines its application.

Introduction of the corrector, universal natural. Denial of the existence of universal natural in the external world. 9. Dealing with the parts of predicate and non-predicate. 10. Distinguishing between external and intellectual composite. 11. Division of composite into real and conventional. Doubt regarding the realness or conventionality of the composition of man. 12. New responses to objections to the definition (of genus). 15. Defense of genus being genus for high, low, medium, and individual genera. Categories. 16. Following the Stoics in restricting categories to four categories. 17. Response to the objection to categories being genera. Differentia. 18. Critique of the definition of 'differentia' in Ibn Sina's Isharat and critique of Khunaji and Irmawi. 19. New critique of the causality of differentia for the specific portion of genus based on Aristotle's view that the body is a condition for the origination of the soul from the Active Intellect. 20. Opposition to Fakhr al-Razi regarding differentia being a property in real quiddities. Definition. 21. Denial of deficient definition being solely differentia. 22. Denial of deficient description being solely property. 23. Acceptance of deficient definition being the combination of differentia and property. 24. Indefinability of conventional composites by genus and differentia. 25. Necessity of composition from genus and differentia for things composed of non-predicate parts. Topic of affirmations. Propositions. Introduction of the corrector. 26. Figurativeness of naming negative and disjunctive propositions. 27. Opposition to Fakhr al-Razi and Khunaji regarding 'subject-ness' and 'predicate-ness' being parts of the proposition. 28. Explicit statement that the real subject is the essence of the subject, not the title 'subject'. 29. Explanation of how it is possible to predicate a description of an essence, like 'Zayd is a writer'. The objection is that predicating two different things is not permissible. Samarqandi's answer is that the two descriptions 'movement' and 'moving' differ; the former cannot be predicated but the latter can, because the latter is in reality 'essence qualified by a description', not just a description, because it means 'essence for which movement is established'. Positive negative predicate. 30. Positive negative predicate: 'Every J is not B' (Khunaji and Irmawi). The term 'positive negative predicate' originates from Khunaji, who introduced it in the discussion of contraposition and real and external propositions. (Kashf al-Asrar, p. 144). Irmawi borrowed this term and used it in discussing Ibn Sina's syllogism: No A is B; everything that is not B is C; therefore every A is C. Irmawi claims that the minor premise is neither a modified affirmative nor a negative, but rather a positive negative predicate. (Irmawi, p. 284). Samarqandi uses this term to distinguish between two meanings of Ibn Sina's sentence: 'Every J is not B' ('Every J is not B'). He says 'Every J is not B' is a universal negative, and 'Every J is not B' [with different phrasing] is a universal negative modified. He uses this distinction in the topic of contraposition to solve some problems...
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents or a list of topics and innovations (nawā'īrī) attributed to Samarqandi in the book Qustas. It is not a continuous lesson but an outline of the subjects covered. The list begins with general topics in logic: the universal natural (kullī ṭabīʿī), composite (murakkab), genus (jins), categories (maqūlāt), differentia (faṣl), definition (taʿrīf), propositions (qaḍāyā), and ends with a discussion of 'positive negative predicate' (mūjiba sāliba al-maḥmūl). The last portion provides a detailed note on the terminology, tracing it to Khunaji and Irmawi, and explaining Samarqandi's use to distinguish between universal negative and modified negative propositions in the context of contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ). Since this is an outline, no argument is presented; rather, it serves as a guide to the book's structure and the key points of innovation.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Overview of Samarqandi's Logical Innovations
List of topics on categorical propositions, conversion, contradiction, etc.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main innovations of Samarqandi in logic as listed in his table of contents and relate them to the classification of propositions and conversion rules.

Core Idea
Samarqandi's innovations refine modal proposition types and conversion rules, building on and critiquing earlier logicians.

This lesson introduces you to the key topics of Samarqandi's logical innovations as outlined in his table of contents. You will see a structured list covering proposition types (external, real, mental), modalities, contradiction, conversion, and conditionals. This overview will help you map the advanced discussions that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proposition types
Notice the classification of propositions into external (khārijiyya), real (ḥaqīqiyya), and mental (dhihniyya). These distinctions are central to Samarqandi's work.
External propositions refer to individuals existing at a specific time; real propositions refer to individuals irrespective of time; mental propositions refer to individuals in the mind.
2
Note the modal distinctions
The list includes essential necessity, essential possibility, and occurrence possibility. These modalities qualify how propositions hold.
3
Recognize the topics on contradiction and conversion
Samarqandi discusses contradiction (tanāquḍ) and two types of conversion: simple conversion ('aks mustawī) and conversion by contrapositive ('aks al-naqīḍ). He also addresses fallacies related to conversion.
4
Observe the critiques of earlier logicians
The table shows disagreements with Khunaji and Fakhr al-Razi, particularly on conversion of universal negative propositions and the example of modal copula.
5
Notice the inclusion of conditionals
The list includes conditional propositions (sharṭiyyāt), including accidental conditionals and causal types such as correlation (taḍāyuf).

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents listing the logical innovations of Samarqandi as discussed in the work. It covers a wide range of topics: the classification of propositions into external (khārijiyya), real (ḥaqīqiyya), and mental (dhihniyya); the necessity of the subject's existence in conditional negatives; modalities such as essential necessity and possibility; contradictions; simple conversion and conversion by contrapositive; and conditional propositions. There are also references to disputes with earlier logicians like Khunaji and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The list serves as an overview of the advanced logical discussions that follow in the main text.

Key Terms

Checkpoint
According to the table of contents, whose response to Fakhr al-Razi's criticism on the conversion of universal negative perpetual real propositions is critiqued?
A) Ibn Sina
B) Samarqandi
C) Khunaji
D) Al-Farabi

Key Takeaway
Samarqandi's innovations systematically refine the classification of propositions, modalities, and conversion rules, responding to earlier scholars like Ibn Sina, Khunaji, and Fakhr al-Razi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the three types of propositions according to Samarqandi and give an example of each from kalam (e.g., 'Every creation is temporally originated' as real, 'Some humans are now writing' as external, 'Pegasus has wings' as mental).
?
Explain why Samarqandi defends mental propositions against critics, and provide a reason why mental propositions are useful in logical reasoning (e.g., for hypothetical reasoning about non-existent entities in theological debates).

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Division of Knowledge: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Editor quotes Samarkandi's definitions of tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq based on the presence or absence of judgment, and identify Samarkandi's definition from Qustas al-Afkar.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either bare conception or conception with judgment.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational division of knowledge (al-'ilm) into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent), as defined by Samarkandi in his Qustas al-Afkar. This division, inherited from al-Farabi and Fakhr al-Razi, shapes the entire subject matter of logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions of al-'ilm in the passage
Find the Arabic lines where Samarkandi defines knowledge as either tasawwur or tasdiq. Notice the phrasing 'in kana idrakan bila al-hukm' (if it is perception without judgment) and 'in kana ma'ahu' (if it is with judgment).
2
Identify the key condition
The presence or absence of judgment (hukm) is what distinguishes tasawwur from tasdiq. Tasawwur is bare perception; tasdiq includes a judgment.
3
Compare with al-Farabi's and Fakhr al-Razi's definitions
The passage notes that Samarkandi's wording is close to Khwaja Nasir al-Din al-Tusi's. Notice how different scholars define the same division.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider the mental act of imagining 'the world' (tasawwur) versus believing 'the world is created' (tasdiq). The latter includes a judgment.

Ibn Badi' Bandehi (10-7192)) 1. Objection to the division of tasawwur and tasdiq into knowledge and ignorance. Handwritten copy no. 1461, Raghib Pasha Library, p. 349, 2-4. 2. Objection to the categorical proposition with disjunctive predicate being a disjunctive proposition (Sharh al-Muqaddima al-Kashiyya, p. 486, line 12-14). Najm al-Din Nakhjivani. Increase of productive moods of the four figures. Athir al-Din al-Abhari. 1. Two definitions for 'knowledge' in the division of tasawwur and tasdiq according to al-Farabi's definition: 'Knowledge is either tasawwur... or tasdiq, and it is the judgment' (Muntaha al-Afkar fi Ibanat al-Asrar, p. 79). And it is said: tasdiq is merely the judgment (Muntaha al-Afkar fi Ibanat al-Asrar, p. 199). According to Fakhr al-Razi's definition: 'Knowledge is either bare tasawwur... or tasawwur with judgment, and the totality is called tasdiq' (Muntaha al-Afkar fi Ibanat al-Asrar, p. 199). 'Knowledge is either only tasawwur... or tasawwur with tasdiq' (Khalasat al-Afkar wa Naqawat al-Asrar, p. 97; Tanzil al-Afkar, pp. 139-140). Samarkandi brings this second definition with an expression close to the expression of Khwaja Nasir in Talkhis al-Muhassal (p. 6), indicating possible influence from this book. Samarkandi's being influenced by predecessors, p. 27: 'Knowledge is either tasawwur if it is perception without judgment, or tasdiq if it is with judgment' (Qustas al-Afkar). 'Knowledge is either tasawwur if judgment is not included in it, or tasdiq if it is included' (Qustas al-Afkar). Simplicity of tasdiq (Muntaha al-Afkar fi Ibanat al-Asrar, pp. 79 and 199). Interpretation of secondary intelligibles as restrictive and assertive judgments (Khalasat al-Afkar wa Naqawat al-Asrar, p. 97). The subject of logic is tasawwurat and tasdiqat (Khalasat al-Afkar wa Naqawat al-Asrar, p. 105; Tanzil al-Afkar, p. 144) following Khunaji in Kashf al-Asrar, pp. 8-9. Resolving the puzzle of absolute unknown by distinguishing the essential proposition and the descriptive proposition (Khalasat al-Afkar wa Naqawat al-Asrar, p. 100, line 1614? 6. Definition of 'mu'arif' (definiens): 'The definiens of the quiddity of a thing is that which necessitates, by its conception, the conception of that quiddity or its distinction from everything else' (al-Abhari, Kashf al-Haqa'iq fi Tahrir al-Daqa'iq, p. 47). The natural proposition (Muntaha al-Afkar, pp. 109 and 216; Khalasat al-Afkar, p. 174). The mental proposition: condition of possibility in the real proposition (Muntaha al-Afkar, pp. 109 and 218; Khalasat al-Afkar, p. 178; Tanzil al-Afkar, p. 161). Denial of mental propositions (Muntaha al-Afkar, p. 109, lines 10-12). 10. Deduction from an extraneous premise (Unwan al-Haqq wa Burhan al-Sidq, p. 591, line 2-12). 11. Response to the objection of Khwajah to al-Abhari regarding acceptance of entailment from whole to part (Ta'dil al-Mi'yar fi Naqd Tanzil al-Afkar, p. 174). 12. Response to al-Abhari's objection to the conditions of truth of conditional (proposition) according to Ibn Sina (Kashf al-Haqa'iq fi Tahrir al-Daqa'iq, p. 72; Muntaha al-Afkar fi Ibanat al-Asrar, pp. 113-114 and 223). 13. The proof of the demonstration of 'inna' in which the middle term is the effect of the minor. 14. Objection to the categorical proposition with disjunctive predicate being a disjunctive proposition (Unwan al-Haqq wa Burhan al-Sidq, p. 589). 15. The rule of preventing estimation (man' al-taqdir) among the dialecticians of the time (Muntaha al-Afkar, p. 254; Kashf al-Haqa'iq, pp. 155-156; Khalasat al-Afkar, p. 323; Tanzil al-Afkar, pp. 213-214). It is well-known among the dialecticians of this time: prevention of matters (man' al-taqdir).
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an editor's introduction (muqaddima musahhih) to Qustas al-Afkar by Samarkandi. It surveys the influences on Samarkandi from earlier logicians and lists many technical topics in logic that he discusses in the work. The editor begins by mentioning that Samarkandi's definitions of knowledge (al-'ilm) as divided into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) follow the definitions of al-Farabi and Fakhr al-Razi, with wording close to that of Khwaja Nasir al-Din al-Tusi in Talkhis al-Muhassal. Two versions of Samarkandi's own definition are quoted from Qustas: 'Knowledge is either tasawwur if it is perception without judgment, or tasdiq if it is with judgment' and 'Knowledge is either tasawwur if judgment is not included in it, or tasdiq if it is included.' The editor then lists numerous issues addressed in Qustas: the simplicity of tasdiq, the interpretation of secondary intelligibles as restrictive and assertive judgments, the subject matter of logic as tasawwurat and tasdiqat (following Khunaji), resolving the puzzle of the absolute unknown by distinguishing essential and descriptive propositions, the definition of mu'arif (definiens) according to al-Abhari, the natural proposition, the mental proposition as a condition for the real proposition, the denial of mental propositions, deduction from an extraneous premise, a response to Khwajah's objection to al-Abhari on entailment from whole to part, a response to al-Abhari's objection to Ibn Sina's conditions for the truth of conditional propositions, the proof of demonstration of 'inna' where the middle term is the effect of the minor, the objection to the categorical proposition with disjunctive predicate being a disjunctive proposition, and the rule of preventing estimation (man' al-taqdir) among dialecticians. This introduction shows that Qustas engages deeply with the preceding logical tradition, especially critiques and refinements of positions by al-Abhari, Ibn Sina, Fakhr al-Razi, and Khwaja Nasir al-Din al-Tusi.

تصديق

Checkpoint
According to Samarkandi's definition in Qustas al-Afkar, what distinguishes tasawwur from tasdiq?
A) The presence of a subject
B) The presence or absence of judgment (hukm)
C) The number of terms
D) The truth value

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq is fundamental to Islamic logic, hinging on the presence of judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an example from Islamic law: identify an act of tasawwur and an act of tasdiq related to the concept of 'zakat'.
?
Explain why the statement 'Allah exists' is a tasdiq, while the mental image of 'Allah' is a tasawwur.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Definiens According to al-Abhari
Editor quotes al-Abhari's definition of mu'arif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state al-Abhari's definition of mu'arif and identify its two key functions: conception and distinction.

Core Idea
A definition must either conceive the quiddity or distinguish it from everything else.

The passage quotes al-Abhari's definition of mu'arif (definiens) from his Kashf al-Haqa'iq. This definition is a crucial tool for constructing valid definitions in logic and law.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Abhari's definition in Arabic
Find the Arabic: 'المعرف لماهية الشيء هو الذي يوجب تصوّره تصوّرها أو تميزها عن كل ما عداها'. Note that it requires either conceiving the quiddity or distinguishing it.
2
Identify the two outcomes
The mu'arif should lead to either tasawwur of the quiddity or tamayyuz (distinction) from others. Are both required? The definition uses 'aw' (or), so one suffices.
3
Compare with hadd and rasm
A 'hadd haqiqi' achieves both conception and distinction; a 'rasm' might only distinguish. This definition allows for both types.
4
Apply to an Islamic concept
Try defining 'wudhu' using al-Abhari's definition: 'Wudhu is washing specific limbs with water.' Does this define the quiddity? Does it distinguish from tayammum?

Definition of 'mu'arif' (definiens): 'The definiens of the quiddity of a thing is that which necessitates, by its conception, the conception of that quiddity or its distinction from everything else' (al-Abhari, Kashf al-Haqa'iq fi Tahrir al-Daqa'iq, p. 47). The natural proposition (Muntaha al-Afkar, pp. 109 and 216; Khalasat al-Afkar, p. 174). The mental proposition: condition of possibility in the real proposition (Muntaha al-Afkar, pp. 109 and 218; Khalasat al-Afkar, p. 178; Tanzil al-Afkar, p. 161). Denial of mental propositions (Muntaha al-Afkar, p. 109, lines 10-12). 10. Deduction from an extraneous premise (Unwan al-Haqq wa Burhan al-Sidq, p. 591, line 2-12). 11. Response to the objection of Khwajah to al-Abhari regarding acceptance of entailment from whole to part (Ta'dil al-Mi'yar fi Naqd Tanzil al-Afkar, p. 174). 12. Response to al-Abhari's objection to the conditions of truth of conditional (proposition) according to Ibn Sina (Kashf al-Haqa'iq fi Tahrir al-Daqa'iq, p. 72; Muntaha al-Afkar fi Ibanat al-Asrar, pp. 113-114 and 223). 13. The proof of the demonstration of 'inna' in which the middle term is the effect of the minor. 14. Objection to the categorical proposition with disjunctive predicate being a disjunctive proposition (Unwan al-Haqq wa Burhan al-Sidq, p. 589). 15. The rule of preventing estimation (man' al-taqdir) among the dialecticians of the time (Muntaha al-Afkar, p. 254; Kashf al-Haqa'iq, pp. 155-156; Khalasat al-Afkar, p. 323; Tanzil al-Afkar, pp. 213-214). It is well-known among the dialecticians of this time: prevention of matters (man' al-taqdir).
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an editor's introduction (muqaddima musahhih) to Qustas al-Afkar by Samarkandi. It surveys the influences on Samarkandi from earlier logicians and lists many technical topics in logic that he discusses in the work. The editor begins by mentioning that Samarkandi's definitions of knowledge (al-'ilm) as divided into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) follow the definitions of al-Farabi and Fakhr al-Razi, with wording close to that of Khwaja Nasir al-Din al-Tusi in Talkhis al-Muhassal. Two versions of Samarkandi's own definition are quoted from Qustas: 'Knowledge is either tasawwur if it is perception without judgment, or tasdiq if it is with judgment' and 'Knowledge is either tasawwur if judgment is not included in it, or tasdiq if it is included.' The editor then lists numerous issues addressed in Qustas: the simplicity of tasdiq, the interpretation of secondary intelligibles as restrictive and assertive judgments, the subject matter of logic as tasawwurat and tasdiqat (following Khunaji), resolving the puzzle of the absolute unknown by distinguishing essential and descriptive propositions, the definition of mu'arif (definiens) according to al-Abhari, the natural proposition, the mental proposition as a condition for the real proposition, the denial of mental propositions, deduction from an extraneous premise, a response to Khwajah's objection to al-Abhari on entailment from whole to part, a response to al-Abhari's objection to Ibn Sina's conditions for the truth of conditional propositions, the proof of demonstration of 'inna' where the middle term is the effect of the minor, the objection to the categorical proposition with disjunctive predicate being a disjunctive proposition, and the rule of preventing estimation (man' al-taqdir) among dialecticians. This introduction shows that Qustas engages deeply with the preceding logical tradition, especially critiques and refinements of positions by al-Abhari, Ibn Sina, Fakhr al-Razi, and Khwaja Nasir al-Din al-Tusi.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Man' al-Taqdir: A Dialectical Principle
Editor notes the dialectical principle of preventing estimation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rule of man' al-taqdir and its use in jadal.

Core Idea
Prevention of estimation: a dialectician's tool to block imaginary counterexamples.

The passage mentions the rule of preventing estimation (man' al-taqdir) as a well-known practice among dialecticians of Samarkandi's time. This principle is used in jadal to reject hypothetical objections that have no real existence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the mention of man' al-taqdir in the passage
Find the Arabic: 'قاعدة منع تقدير نزد جدليان زمان' and 'اشتهر بين الجدليين في هذا الزمان منع الأمور (منع التقدير)'. This indicates the rule was common.
2
Understand the function
Man' al-taqdir means preventing hypothetical estimation (taqdir). In debate, if an objector proposes an imaginary scenario to challenge a claim, the respondent may invoke man' al-taqdir to dismiss it as irrelevant or unreal.
3
Consider a jadal example
Suppose a claim: 'Alcohol is forbidden because it intoxicates'. An objector says 'What if there is an alcohol that does not intoxicate?' The respondent uses man' al-taqdir: 'Such alcohol is not estimated (maqdir) in reality; it is a mere supposition.'
4
Evaluate its strength
Man' al-taqdir is a procedural rule, not a logical necessity. It avoids infinite regress of hypothetical objections but must be used carefully.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Tracing the Chain of a Logical Text
Editor discusses two editions of Qustas and identifies the first treatise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the transmission of a key logical text from its author through later editions, understanding the importance of manuscript verification.

Core Idea
Knowing the chain of transmission (isnad) of a scholarly work is as essential in logic as in hadith.

When you encounter a logical work like al-Risala al-Shamsiyya, it is not enough to know its title and author. You must understand how it reached you: through which manuscripts, editions, and editors. This lesson uses the editor's introduction to Qustas al-Afkar to illustrate the rigorous approach needed to authenticate a text before using it as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the work and its author
Locate the title of the treatise (al-Risala al-Shamsiyya) and the full name of its author (Najm al-Din al-Katibi). Note the patronymic chain (ibn Bakr ibn Ali ibn Umar) — this is part of the isnad tradition.
Knowing the full chain of names helps avoid confusion with other scholars.
2
Determine the context of the passage
This is not the treatise itself, but an editor's introduction listing contents. The editor mentions two editions: one by Sprenger and one by Najm al-Din Pahlawan. Ask: why does the editor discuss multiple editions?
Editors compare editions to produce a corrected text (tashih).
3
Assess the editor’s role and reliability
The editor is unnamed in this fragment, but he provides provenance. In Islamic scholarship, the editor's chain of transmission (rawi) matters. Check if the editor is known for accuracy.
This parallels the practice in hadith criticism (jarh wa ta‘dil).
4
Apply the same scrutiny to any logical text
Before citing a logical argument from al-Risala al-Shamsiyya, verify which edition you are using. Compare marginal notes (hawashi) and check for interpolations.
Philosophical errors often creep in through corrupted manuscripts.

Source Text

الرسالة الأولى و هي الرسالة الشمسية

بن عمر الكاتبي القزويني

The editor mentions that Sprenger published Qustas alongside this appendix, and later discusses the edition by Najm al-Din Pahlawan.

Key Takeaway
Every logical text you use has a transmission history; verifying it is the first step in intellectual responsibility.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Reading Editorial Corrections in Islamic Manuscripts
Editor critiques a previous edition and lists textual corrections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish editorial front matter from the main text and interpret a table of textual corrections.

Core Idea
Editorial corrections preserve textual integrity but are not the logical argument.

This passage is not a logical lesson but the editor's introduction correcting errors in a previous edition. Understanding how to read such editorial apparatus is crucial for anyone working with primary Islamic texts. Here, the editor lists dozens of corrections, typically showing the mistaken reading followed by the correct one.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the genre
Notice the heading 'مقدمة مصحح' (Editor's Introduction). This signals that you are reading editorial front matter, not the main text.
Front matter often contains corrections, introductions, or biographical notes.
2
Parse the table structure
The text lists pairs of words: the first is the error, the second is the correction, separated by a slash. For example, 'نقيض / نقيضي' means the word should be 'نقيضي' not 'نقيض'.
The slash (/) separates the mistaken reading from the corrected reading.
3
Identify the type of corrections
Corrections range from spelling (e.g., 'بالكلي / بالكلية') to grammatical forms (e.g., 'دائمیا / دائمین') and even whole words (e.g., 'الأفراد / الإفراد').
Some corrections affect logical terminology, like 'نقيض' vs. 'نقيضي'.
4
Understand the purpose
These corrections ensure future readers use the accurate wording of the logical text. Without them, mistakes in a previous edition could lead to misunderstanding the author's argument.
Textual criticism is vital in Islamic manuscript traditions.

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction (مقدمة مصحح). It is not the main logical text of Qustas. The editor critiques a previous edition by Pahlavan, listing numerous errors in word readings and variant readings. The table provides corrections for many terms, e.g., 'نقيض' should be 'نقيضي', 'بالكلي' should be 'بالكلية'. These corrections are important for textual accuracy but do not constitute a logical lesson. The student should note that this is front matter, not a logical argument or definition.

Introduction and Front Matter

Textual Criticism in Islamic Logic Manuscripts
Editor identifies and corrects textual errors in an earlier edition of Qustas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify common types of textual errors in Islamic logic manuscripts and evaluate their impact on meaning.

Core Idea
Textual precision safeguards logical meaning—every scribal error can distort a definition.

Even authoritative works like al-Ghazali's Qustas al-Fikr can suffer from scribal errors in transmission. Editors, by comparing manuscripts, restore the author's intended reading. This lesson shows how a modern editor corrects three types of errors: pronoun gender mismatch, missing conjunction, and misinterpreted clause structure. You will learn to spot similar issues in Arabic logical texts and assess their effect on the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Error Type
Read the edited passage and the commentary. The editor points out three corrections: a gender mismatch in pronouns, a missing conjunction (wa) that changes clause interpretation, and a clarification of a compound expression's signification. Note the specific textual issue in each case.
Focus on the first three corrections; the fourth is incomplete due to OCR.
2
Locate the Textual Discrepancy
Find the Arabic phrase where the error occurred in the earlier edition. For example, the editor notes that 'سهل الزوال كان أو عسره، سريعه أو بطيئه' had mismatched pronouns. Compare the corrected reading in the given passage.
Use the translation and commentary to confirm the exact words.
3
Determine the Correct Reading
From the editor's reasoning and manuscript evidence, decide which reading preserves al-Ghazali's intended meaning. For the gender error, the pronoun should refer to 'zawal' (removal), not 'salaha' (facilitation).
The editor's corrections are based on manuscript consensus.
4
Assess Impact on Logical Meaning
Explain how the error would mislead a reader. For the conjunction error, without 'wa', a student might think 'يتعذر الوقوف عليه' is a description of 'تكلف' (artificial effort), which would confuse the classification of non-necessary accidents.
Logical precision relies on correct clause relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغير اللازم: إما مفارق بالقوة كـ «سواد الحبشي»، أو [مفارق] بالفعل: سهل الزوال كان أو عسره، سريعه أو بطيئه.
يكون بحيث إذا سُمِعَ عُلم من غير تكلّف ويتعذر الوقوف عليه ما لم يُسمع.
ويكفي تصوّرها بوجه ما، كما نحكم على جسم معين بأنه شاغل لحيز معين وليس بشاغل لحيز آخر.
و دلالة لفظ المركب داخلة في المطابقة، إذ المعنى من وضع اللفظ للمعنى وضع عينه لعينه أو أجزائه لأجزائه بحيث يطابق أجزاء اللفظ أجزاء المعنى.
And the non-necessary (accident): either separable potentially like 'the blackness of the Ethiopian', or separable actually: either easy of removal or difficult, quick or slow. It is such that when heard it is understood without artificial effort, and it is impossible to grasp it without having heard it. And it suffices to conceive them in some way, as we judge of a particular body that it occupies a particular space and does not occupy another space. And the signification of a compound expression is included in correspondence (mutabaqa), because the meaning from the placing of an expression for a meaning is the placing of its whole for the whole or its parts for its parts, such that the parts of the expression correspond to the parts of the meaning.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents editorial corrections to an earlier printed edition (تصحیح پهلوان) of Qustas. The editor, noting discrepancies in the original, restores readings from manuscripts. The first correction addresses the classification of 'non-necessary' (غير اللازم) accidents: the earlier edition had mismatched gender in 'سهل الزوال كان أو عسره، سريعه أو بطيئه'; the correct form links the pronouns to 'زوال' (removal). The second correction shows that the phrase 'يتعذر الوقوف عليه ما لم يسمع' (it is impossible to grasp it without having heard it) is not a description of 'تكلّف' (artificial effort) but an independent clause conjoined by 'واو'. The third correction clarifies that 'ليس بشاغل لحيز آخر' is not a qualifier of 'حيز معين' but conjoined to the preceding clause to describe the body. The fourth correction, though incomplete in the OCR, concerns compound expression (لفظ مركب) and its correspondence (مطابقة) to meaning: the phrase should read 'يطابق أجزاء اللفظ أجزاء المعنى' (the parts of the expression correspond to the parts of the meaning). These corrections are crucial for preserving the logical precision of al-Ghazali's text.

Key Takeaway
Textual errors like pronoun mismatches and missing conjunctions can distort logical definitions; editors restore authorial intent through manuscript comparison.

Evaluating Manuscript Evidence in Textual Criticism
Editor discusses lacunae and features of manuscripts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify features of a manuscript that affect its value for establishing a critical edition of an Islamic text.

Core Idea
A critical edition depends on careful evaluation of manuscript lacunae, errors, and relationships.

When preparing a critical edition of an Islamic work like Qustas al-Afkar, the editor must assess each manuscript's completeness, accuracy, and genealogical relationship to others. This lesson walks through the editor's analysis of three manuscripts of Qustas al-Afkar, showing how manuscript features determine their role in the edition.

Guided Path
1
Identify manuscript lacunae
Look for missing portions at beginning or end of the manuscript. The Astan Quds copy lacks about three lines at the start and two-sevenths at the end.
2
Detect duplication errors
The Astan Quds copy has a repeated section due to a folio error. Recognize how such errors occur and affect the text.
3
Assess scribal accuracy
Compare error rates: the Astan Quds copy has few errors, while the Majlis copy is highly erroneous.
4
Determine manuscript relationships
Shared errors and missing portions suggest common ancestry. The editor deduces that the Astan Quds and Majlis copies derive from a lost archetype.
5
Evaluate editorial reliance
The editor uses the Astan Quds copy as primary source, supplements with Pahlavan's edition for missing parts, and avoids the faulty Majlis copy.
